{"uri": "8251650777", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-31", "time": "03:14:03", "dateTime": "2024-07-31T03:14:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-31T03:12:57Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8274509906768799, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/partners/ap/national/harris-is-planning-a-tour-of-battleground-states-next-week-with-her-yet-to-be/article_e68444f5-3a88-5fa0-84cc-ba5a733eb0d7.html", "title": "Harris is planning a tour of battleground states next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate", "body": "Michigan Gov. Gretchen Whitmer meets with attendees during campaign event for Democratic presidential candidate Vice President Kamala Harris in Ambler, Pa., Monday, July 29, 2024.\n\nBy BILL BARROW, STEVE PEOPLES and ZEKE MILLER - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Vice President Kamala Harris is set to launch a battleground tour next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate, with stops in seven swing states stretching from Pennsylvania to Nevada, her campaign said Tuesday.\n\nThe planned tour is the latest sign of the whirlwind pace at which Harris has gone from President Joe Biden's supportive running mate to likely Democratic nominee ready to identify her No. 2 and take on Republican Donald Trump and Ohio Sen. JD Vance.\n\nHarris said Tuesday she hadn't made a decision yet on whom she will select.\n\nThose who are under consideration have been demonstrating a time-honored tradition: summertime auditions in which vice presidential contenders walk the line between open self-promotion and loyal advocacy for the potential boss.\n\nTo that end, Democrat Josh Shapiro told enthusiastic voters in suburban Philadelphia this week that Harris belongs in the White House -- and then reminded them of all he's done as governor of battleground Pennsylvania. Kentucky Gov. Andy Beshear, likewise, told voters in Georgia that Harris has the makings of \"a great president\" -- and then highlighted the elections he's won as a Democrat in Republican territory.\n\nPeople are also reading... Sierra signage going up at former Sioux City Bed Bath & Beyond North Sioux City crash kills one 4-year-old boy escaped from cage in Iowa home; deputies found him covered in feces on street Sioux City's Lyons Park getting $1.22 million in upgrades BNSF Railway wants to remove collapsed Big Sioux River train bridge Now streaming and on DVD: 'The Richmond Way' gives us the whole 'Ted Lasso' story Jason's Frozen Treats open second dine-in ice cream parlor Body found in Floyd River in Sioux City Sioux City driver charged with vehicular homicide from weekend crash Elk Point man killed in North Sioux City crash Tom and Molly Bedell's West Lake Okoboji home sells for state record $9.5M Wife of Dakota Dunes fatal stabbing suspect charged as an accessory Financial situation for McCook Lake residents still murky a month after flood State baseball: Scrapy dynamic duo sends Crusaders to state title game, down top seed Wahlert Woodbury County Court Report for July 22, 2024\n\nHarris' campaign has been vetting about a dozen potential running mates, according to people familiar with the search process. Shapiro and Arizona Sen. Mark Kelly are seen as among the front-runners, according to the people.\n\nThe campaign said Harris and her running mate will make stops next week in Philadelphia; western Wisconsin; Detroit; Raleigh, North Carolina; Savannah, Georgia; Phoenix and Las Vegas.\n\nIn the meantime, Harris advisers, led by former Attorney General Eric Holder, have been combing through reams of paperwork submitted by potential running mates, while the candidate herself is holding personal conversations with the finalists, according to a person familiar with the matter who spoke on the condition of anonymity to discuss details that haven't been made public.\n\nHarris, according to another person familiar with the matter, is seeking someone with executive experience who can also serve as a governing partner. Notions of a so-called short list have not stopped those on the Democrats' broader national bench from finding the spotlight.\n\n\"I'm not going to talk about the interactions I've had with the campaign,\" Illinois Gov. J.B. Pritzker declared recently on MSNBC. He added, though, \"Let's just say I'm aware that the vetting process is quite an in depth one.\" Then he listed his accomplishments, offering that he was the only Midwestern governor to raise his state minimum wage to $15 per hour.\n\nHarris would be the first woman, first Black woman and first person of South Asian descent to serve as president. Many Democrats have argued she should balance her ticket both demographically and politically.\n\nDems' VP list has notable differences\n\nShapiro, 51, is among the most popular U.S. governors, winning his 2022 election in a rout over a Trump-endorsed Republican. He's an outspoken supporter of abortion rights who has won three statewide elections in Pennsylvania. His speaking style draws comparisons to former President Barack Obama. But he has taken flak from the left for his support for Israel's war on Hamas, a private school voucher program and natural gas infrastructure.\n\nHis allies argue that he would help Harris win Pennsylvania, complicating if not blocking Trump's path to an Electoral College majority.\n\nLike all contenders, Shapiro sidesteps questions about the vetting process and stresses Harris should not be pressured. But he's mentioned more than once that he's known her for nearly two decades.\n\nBeshear stands out in a heavily Republican state. During his weekend stop in Georgia, he talked of winning votes in \"tough counties\" but emphasized liberal bona fides: \"I am a proud pro-union governor. I am a proud pro-choice governor. I am a proud public education governor. I am a proud pro-diversity governor.\"\n\nClosest in age to JD Vance among the Democrats' possibilities, Beshear openly mocks Trump's understudy for presenting himself as a son of Appalachia. \"I mean, there's a county that JD Vance says he's from in Kentucky - and I won it by 22 points last November,\" he said.\n\nBeshear and Shapiro were both state attorneys general, like Harris, before becoming governors. But their tenures did not overlap considerably with Harris' service in California. She worked more closely with North Carolina Gov. Roy Cooper during his days as an attorney general, but Cooper on Monday said he had opted not to be considered for vice president.\n\nMinnesota Gov. Tim Walz, 60, is a favorite of some progressives. He brings an atypical national political resume: He was a non-commissioned Army officer, public school teacher and state high school championship football coach before entering politics. Before being elected governor, he was one of the last white Democrats in Congress to represent a mostly rural, small-town House district -- a notable juxtaposition to Harris, the Bay Area Californian.\n\n\"She will make the best choice she's going to,\" Walz said Sunday on CNN, a day after Trump held a mass rally in St. Cloud, Minnesota. \"One way or another, she is going to win in November, and that's going to benefit everyone,\" Walz said, including \"a lot of those folks who were out in St. Cloud with the (former) president.\"\n\nKelly, 60, is the only top contender in Congress. He boasts an impressive military resume and experience as an astronaut. He has strong Latino support locally and solid relationships with Arizona officials along the U.S.-Mexico border. That balance could give him credibility on immigration policy as Republicans frame high numbers of migrant border crossings as a national crisis.\n\nBut Kelly has had to shore up his credentials with labor, a key Democratic faction. He took heat from union leaders because he was one of just a handful of Democrats who did not sponsor the Protecting the Right to Organize (PRO) Act, which would make it easier to organize workers. He said at the time he supported the goals but had concerns. Following pressure this month, he now says he would vote for the bill if it came up for a vote.\n\nEveryone has an opinion\n\nWhile Harris considers her choices, everyone seems to have an opinion.\n\nSteven Benjamin, the White House director of public engagement, laughed as he told reporters on Air Force One on Monday that his office has received thousands of recommendations from around the country.\n\nDonna Brazile, who managed Democrat Al Gore's presidential campaign in 2000 and was instrumental in urging Biden to pick Harris in 2020, said the selection process involves \"a lot of noise\" that underplays the complexity of the decision.\n\n\"The most important stage is what the lawyers will do to you,\" she said, with a laugh and emphasizing the seriousness. \"It's worse than a dental hygiene check. ... Before you get to suitability and other factors, before it gets to political people like me, they've done a forensic examination of your life.\"\n\nBarrow reported from Cumming, Georgia. Associated Press reporters Will Weissert in Washington, Gary Robertson in Raleigh, North Carolina; Marc Levy in Harrisburg, Pennsylvania; Bruce Schreiner in Frankfort, Kentucky; Jonathan J. Cooper in Phoenix; and Colleen Long aboard Air Force One contributed.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. {"uri": "8251637023", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-31", "time": "02:59:12", "dateTime": "2024-07-31T02:59:12Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-31T02:58:07Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8235294222831726, "url": "https://pantagraph.com/partners/ap/national/harris-is-planning-a-tour-of-battleground-states-next-week-with-her-yet-to-be/article_436a9ddb-da88-58c3-8894-3b1fb76c42df.html", "title": "Harris is planning a tour of battleground states next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate", "body": "Michigan Gov. Gretchen Whitmer meets with attendees during campaign event for Democratic presidential candidate Vice President Kamala Harris in Ambler, Pa., Monday, July 29, 2024.\n\nBy BILL BARROW, STEVE PEOPLES and ZEKE MILLER - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Vice President Kamala Harris is set to launch a battleground tour next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate, with stops in seven swing states stretching from Pennsylvania to Nevada, her campaign said Tuesday.\n\nThe planned tour is the latest sign of the whirlwind pace at which Harris has gone from President Joe Biden's supportive running mate to likely Democratic nominee ready to identify her No. 2 and take on Republican Donald Trump and Ohio Sen. JD Vance.\n\nHarris said Tuesday she hadn't made a decision yet on whom she will select.\n\nThose who are under consideration have been demonstrating a time-honored tradition: summertime auditions in which vice presidential contenders walk the line between open self-promotion and loyal advocacy for the potential boss.\n\nTo that end, Democrat Josh Shapiro told enthusiastic voters in suburban Philadelphia this week that Harris belongs in the White House -- and then reminded them of all he's done as governor of battleground Pennsylvania. Kentucky Gov. Andy Beshear, likewise, told voters in Georgia that Harris has the makings of \"a great president\" -- and then highlighted the elections he's won as a Democrat in Republican territory.\n\nPeople are also reading... Bloomington approves $6.8M contract for roadwork McLean County Fair selects 2024 royalty Bloomington man denied release from treatment center 15 years after infant daughter's death Bear seen in Illinois likely swam across Ohio River from Kentucky, wildlife official says Rosie's Pub owner leaves beloved downtown business after 21 years REO Speedwagon, Loverboy to perform in Bloomington this fall 2 suspects arrested after shots fired on Morris Avenue in Bloomington Bloomington businessman giving backpacks to families in need Bloomington woman pleads guilty to burglary charge in McLean County Normal police investigating shots fired Tuesday night Records show deputy's behavior, integrity questioned years before shooting of Sonya Massey UPDATE: Missing teen from Normal found safe Riverdale man gets 123 years for 2020 murder in Bloomington Normal man pleads guilty, sentenced for reckless driving in crash that injured 2 students Funerals pending for July 27\n\nHarris' campaign has been vetting about a dozen potential running mates, according to people familiar with the search process. Shapiro and Arizona Sen. Mark Kelly are seen as among the front-runners, according to the people.\n\nThe campaign said Harris and her running mate will make stops next week in Philadelphia; western Wisconsin; Detroit; Raleigh, North Carolina; Savannah, Georgia; Phoenix and Las Vegas.\n\nIn the meantime, Harris advisers, led by former Attorney General Eric Holder, have been combing through reams of paperwork submitted by potential running mates, while the candidate herself is holding personal conversations with the finalists, according to a person familiar with the matter who spoke on the condition of anonymity to discuss details that haven't been made public.\n\nHarris, according to another person familiar with the matter, is seeking someone with executive experience who can also serve as a governing partner. Notions of a so-called short list have not stopped those on the Democrats' broader national bench from finding the spotlight.\n\n\"I'm not going to talk about the interactions I've had with the campaign,\" Illinois Gov. J.B. Pritzker declared recently on MSNBC. He added, though, \"Let's just say I'm aware that the vetting process is quite an in depth one.\" Then he listed his accomplishments, offering that he was the only Midwestern governor to raise his state minimum wage to $15 per hour.\n\nHarris would be the first woman, first Black woman and first person of South Asian descent to serve as president. Many Democrats have argued she should balance her ticket both demographically and politically.\n\nDems' VP list has notable differences\n\nShapiro, 51, is among the most popular U.S. governors, winning his 2022 election in a rout over a Trump-endorsed Republican. He's an outspoken supporter of abortion rights who has won three statewide elections in Pennsylvania. His speaking style draws comparisons to former President Barack Obama. But he has taken flak from the left for his support for Israel's war on Hamas, a private school voucher program and natural gas infrastructure.\n\nHis allies argue that he would help Harris win Pennsylvania, complicating if not blocking Trump's path to an Electoral College majority.\n\nLike all contenders, Shapiro sidesteps questions about the vetting process and stresses Harris should not be pressured. But he's mentioned more than once that he's known her for nearly two decades.\n\nBeshear stands out in a heavily Republican state. During his weekend stop in Georgia, he talked of winning votes in \"tough counties\" but emphasized liberal bona fides: \"I am a proud pro-union governor. I am a proud pro-choice governor. I am a proud public education governor. I am a proud pro-diversity governor.\"\n\nClosest in age to JD Vance among the Democrats' possibilities, Beshear openly mocks Trump's understudy for presenting himself as a son of Appalachia. \"I mean, there's a county that JD Vance says he's from in Kentucky - and I won it by 22 points last November,\" he said.\n\nBeshear and Shapiro were both state attorneys general, like Harris, before becoming governors. But their tenures did not overlap considerably with Harris' service in California. She worked more closely with North Carolina Gov. Roy Cooper during his days as an attorney general, but Cooper on Monday said he had opted not to be considered for vice president.\n\nMinnesota Gov. Tim Walz, 60, is a favorite of some progressives. He brings an atypical national political resume: He was a non-commissioned Army officer, public school teacher and state high school championship football coach before entering politics. Before being elected governor, he was one of the last white Democrats in Congress to represent a mostly rural, small-town House district -- a notable juxtaposition to Harris, the Bay Area Californian.\n\n\"She will make the best choice she's going to,\" Walz said Sunday on CNN, a day after Trump held a mass rally in St. Cloud, Minnesota. \"One way or another, she is going to win in November, and that's going to benefit everyone,\" Walz said, including \"a lot of those folks who were out in St. Cloud with the (former) president.\"\n\nKelly, 60, is the only top contender in Congress. He boasts an impressive military resume and experience as an astronaut. He has strong Latino support locally and solid relationships with Arizona officials along the U.S.-Mexico border. That balance could give him credibility on immigration policy as Republicans frame high numbers of migrant border crossings as a national crisis.\n\nBut Kelly has had to shore up his credentials with labor, a key Democratic faction. He took heat from union leaders because he was one of just a handful of Democrats who did not sponsor the Protecting the Right to Organize (PRO) Act, which would make it easier to organize workers. He said at the time he supported the goals but had concerns. Following pressure this month, he now says he would vote for the bill if it came up for a vote.\n\nEveryone has an opinion\n\nWhile Harris considers her choices, everyone seems to have an opinion.\n\nSteven Benjamin, the White House director of public engagement, laughed as he told reporters on Air Force One on Monday that his office has received thousands of recommendations from around the country.\n\nDonna Brazile, who managed Democrat Al Gore's presidential campaign in 2000 and was instrumental in urging Biden to pick Harris in 2020, said the selection process involves \"a lot of noise\" that underplays the complexity of the decision.\n\n\"The most important stage is what the lawyers will do to you,\" she said, with a laugh and emphasizing the seriousness. \"It's worse than a dental hygiene check. ... Before you get to suitability and other factors, before it gets to political people like me, they've done a forensic examination of your life.\"\n\nBarrow reported from Cumming, Georgia. Associated Press reporters Will Weissert in Washington, Gary Robertson in Raleigh, North Carolina; Marc Levy in Harrisburg, Pennsylvania; Bruce Schreiner in Frankfort, Kentucky; Jonathan J. Cooper in Phoenix; and Colleen Long aboard Air Force One contributed.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. He boasts an impressive military resume and experience as an astronaut. He has strong Latino support locally and solid relationships with Arizona officials along the U.S.-Mexico border. That balance could give him credibility on immigration policy as Republicans frame high numbers of migrant border crossings as a national crisis.\n\nBut Kelly has had to shore up his credentials with labor, a key Democratic faction. He took heat from union leaders because he was one of just a handful of Democrats who did not sponsor the Protecting the Right to Organize (PRO) Act, which would make it easier to organize workers. He said at the time he supported the goals but had concerns. Following pressure this month, he now says he would vote for the bill if it came up for a vote.\n\nEveryone has an opinion\n\nWhile Harris considers her choices, everyone seems to have an opinion.\n\nSteven Benjamin, the White House director of public engagement, laughed as he told reporters on Air Force One on Monday that his office has received thousands of recommendations from around the country.\n\nDonna Brazile, who managed Democrat Al Gore's presidential campaign in 2000 and was instrumental in urging Biden to pick Harris in 2020, said the selection process involves \"a lot of noise\" that underplays the complexity of the decision.\n\n\"The most important stage is what the lawyers will do to you,\" she said, with a laugh and emphasizing the seriousness. \"It's worse than a dental hygiene check. ... Before you get to suitability and other factors, before it gets to political people like me, they've done a forensic examination of your life.\"\n\nBarrow reported from Cumming, Georgia. Associated Press reporters Will Weissert in Washington, Gary Robertson in Raleigh, North Carolina; Marc Levy in Harrisburg, Pennsylvania; Bruce Schreiner in Frankfort, Kentucky; Jonathan J. Cooper in Phoenix; and Colleen Long aboard Air Force One contributed.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. {"uri": "8251637208", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-31", "time": "03:00:56", "dateTime": "2024-07-31T03:00:56Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-31T02:58:15Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8078431487083435, "url": "https://wcfcourier.com/partners/ap/national/harris-is-planning-a-tour-of-battleground-states-next-week-with-her-yet-to-be/article_88d97539-7d01-5e51-9f97-1ff485e8e633.html", "title": "Harris is planning a tour of battleground states next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate", "body": "Michigan Gov. Gretchen Whitmer meets with attendees during campaign event for Democratic presidential candidate Vice President Kamala Harris in Ambler, Pa., Monday, July 29, 2024.\n\nBy BILL BARROW, STEVE PEOPLES and ZEKE MILLER - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Vice President Kamala Harris is set to launch a battleground tour next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate, with stops in seven swing states stretching from Pennsylvania to Nevada, her campaign said Tuesday.\n\nThe planned tour is the latest sign of the whirlwind pace at which Harris has gone from President Joe Biden's supportive running mate to likely Democratic nominee ready to identify her No. 2 and take on Republican Donald Trump and Ohio Sen. JD Vance.\n\nHarris said Tuesday she hadn't made a decision yet on whom she will select.\n\nThose who are under consideration have been demonstrating a time-honored tradition: summertime auditions in which vice presidential contenders walk the line between open self-promotion and loyal advocacy for the potential boss.\n\nTo that end, Democrat Josh Shapiro told enthusiastic voters in suburban Philadelphia this week that Harris belongs in the White House -- and then reminded them of all he's done as governor of battleground Pennsylvania. Kentucky Gov. Andy Beshear, likewise, told voters in Georgia that Harris has the makings of \"a great president\" -- and then highlighted the elections he's won as a Democrat in Republican territory.\n\nPeople are also reading... Waterloo traffic cameras adjust ticket threshold by 1 mph NEW DETAILS: Deere offers statement on severance package for salaried employees let go Wednesday in Waterloo UPDATE: 69 John Deere Waterloo salaried employees let go Wednesday, 34 in Dubuque Waterloo Christian School makes offer on old Cedar Falls High School 4-year-old boy escaped from cage in Iowa home; deputies found him covered in feces on street Waterloo man arrested for trying to run over women Chased out of Buchanan County, Balance music festival coming to Waterloo Waterloo man arrested following short chase Oelwein man dead in two-vehicle crash southwest of Hazleton Neighbors, crews rescue man from sinking vehicle Olympics: Independence wrestling coaches will serve as volunteers at Paris Games Deere held employee meeting Wednesday, no word yet on potential layoffs Waterloo man sentenced to prison for sawed-off shotgun 67 Johnston workers added to Deere layoffs list, bringing Iowa total to 170 this week John Deere idled more than 300 workers in Quad Cities on Wednesday, bringing last week's total to 489\n\nHarris' campaign has been vetting about a dozen potential running mates, according to people familiar with the search process. Shapiro and Arizona Sen. Mark Kelly are seen as among the front-runners, according to the people.\n\nThe campaign said Harris and her running mate will make stops next week in Philadelphia; western Wisconsin; Detroit; Raleigh, North Carolina; Savannah, Georgia; Phoenix and Las Vegas.\n\nIn the meantime, Harris advisers, led by former Attorney General Eric Holder, have been combing through reams of paperwork submitted by potential running mates, while the candidate herself is holding personal conversations with the finalists, according to a person familiar with the matter who spoke on the condition of anonymity to discuss details that haven't been made public.\n\nHarris, according to another person familiar with the matter, is seeking someone with executive experience who can also serve as a governing partner. Notions of a so-called short list have not stopped those on the Democrats' broader national bench from finding the spotlight.\n\n\"I'm not going to talk about the interactions I've had with the campaign,\" Illinois Gov. J.B. Pritzker declared recently on MSNBC. He added, though, \"Let's just say I'm aware that the vetting process is quite an in depth one.\" Then he listed his accomplishments, offering that he was the only Midwestern governor to raise his state minimum wage to $15 per hour.\n\nHarris would be the first woman, first Black woman and first person of South Asian descent to serve as president. Many Democrats have argued she should balance her ticket both demographically and politically.\n\nDems' VP list has notable differences\n\nShapiro, 51, is among the most popular U.S. governors, winning his 2022 election in a rout over a Trump-endorsed Republican. He's an outspoken supporter of abortion rights who has won three statewide elections in Pennsylvania. His speaking style draws comparisons to former President Barack Obama. But he has taken flak from the left for his support for Israel's war on Hamas, a private school voucher program and natural gas infrastructure.\n\nHis allies argue that he would help Harris win Pennsylvania, complicating if not blocking Trump's path to an Electoral College majority.\n\nLike all contenders, Shapiro sidesteps questions about the vetting process and stresses Harris should not be pressured. But he's mentioned more than once that he's known her for nearly two decades.\n\nBeshear stands out in a heavily Republican state. During his weekend stop in Georgia, he talked of winning votes in \"tough counties\" but emphasized liberal bona fides: \"I am a proud pro-union governor. I am a proud pro-choice governor. I am a proud public education governor. I am a proud pro-diversity governor.\"\n\nClosest in age to JD Vance among the Democrats' possibilities, Beshear openly mocks Trump's understudy for presenting himself as a son of Appalachia. \"I mean, there's a county that JD Vance says he's from in Kentucky - and I won it by 22 points last November,\" he said.\n\nBeshear and Shapiro were both state attorneys general, like Harris, before becoming governors. But their tenures did not overlap considerably with Harris' service in California. She worked more closely with North Carolina Gov. Roy Cooper during his days as an attorney general, but Cooper on Monday said he had opted not to be considered for vice president.\n\nMinnesota Gov. Tim Walz, 60, is a favorite of some progressives. He brings an atypical national political resume: He was a non-commissioned Army officer, public school teacher and state high school championship football coach before entering politics. Before being elected governor, he was one of the last white Democrats in Congress to represent a mostly rural, small-town House district -- a notable juxtaposition to Harris, the Bay Area Californian.\n\n\"She will make the best choice she's going to,\" Walz said Sunday on CNN, a day after Trump held a mass rally in St. Cloud, Minnesota. \"One way or another, she is going to win in November, and that's going to benefit everyone,\" Walz said, including \"a lot of those folks who were out in St. Cloud with the (former) president.\"\n\nKelly, 60, is the only top contender in Congress. He boasts an impressive military resume and experience as an astronaut. He has strong Latino support locally and solid relationships with Arizona officials along the U.S.-Mexico border. That balance could give him credibility on immigration policy as Republicans frame high numbers of migrant border crossings as a national crisis.\n\nBut Kelly has had to shore up his credentials with labor, a key Democratic faction. He took heat from union leaders because he was one of just a handful of Democrats who did not sponsor the Protecting the Right to Organize (PRO) Act, which would make it easier to organize workers. He said at the time he supported the goals but had concerns. Following pressure this month, he now says he would vote for the bill if it came up for a vote.\n\nEveryone has an opinion\n\nWhile Harris considers her choices, everyone seems to have an opinion.\n\nSteven Benjamin, the White House director of public engagement, laughed as he told reporters on Air Force One on Monday that his office has received thousands of recommendations from around the country.\n\nDonna Brazile, who managed Democrat Al Gore's presidential campaign in 2000 and was instrumental in urging Biden to pick Harris in 2020, said the selection process involves \"a lot of noise\" that underplays the complexity of the decision.\n\n\"The most important stage is what the lawyers will do to you,\" she said, with a laugh and emphasizing the seriousness. \"It's worse than a dental hygiene check. ... Before you get to suitability and other factors, before it gets to political people like me, they've done a forensic examination of your life.\"\n\nBarrow reported from Cumming, Georgia. Cooper in Phoenix; and Colleen Long aboard Air Force One contributed.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. {"uri": "2024-07-438947688", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-31", "time": "02:46:39", "dateTime": "2024-07-31T02:46:39Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-31T02:10:34Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.800000011920929, "url": "https://www.nwitimes.com/news/state-and-regional/illinois/harris-is-planning-a-tour-of-battleground-states-next-week-with-her-yet-to-be/article_dfb1f85f-5826-56ef-b15f-e7cb516442dc.html", "title": "Harris is planning a tour of battleground states next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate", "body": "By BILL BARROW, STEVE PEOPLES and ZEKE MILLER - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Vice President Kamala Harris is set to launch a battleground tour next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate, with stops in seven swing states stretching from Pennsylvania to Nevada, her campaign said Tuesday.\n\nThe planned tour is the latest sign of the whirlwind pace at which Harris has gone from President Joe Biden's supportive running mate to likely Democratic nominee ready to identify her No. 2 and take on Republican Donald Trump and Ohio Sen. JD Vance.\n\nHarris said Tuesday she hadn't made a decision yet on whom she will select.\n\nThose who are under consideration have been demonstrating a time-honored tradition: summertime auditions in which vice presidential contenders walk the line between open self-promotion and loyal advocacy for the potential boss.\n\nTo that end, Democrat Josh Shapiro told enthusiastic voters in suburban Philadelphia this week that Harris belongs in the White House -- and then reminded them of all he's done as governor of battleground Pennsylvania. Kentucky Gov. Andy Beshear, likewise, told voters in Georgia that Harris has the makings of \"a great president\" -- and then highlighted the elections he's won as a Democrat in Republican territory.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nValparaiso University to eliminate 30 academic programs Davich: 'Mr. Safety' killed at dangerous bike trail intersection in Region Valpo jewelry store burglarized; holes cut in wall, cops say Mascot Hall of Fame to close in September, 'going out with confetti' Former U.S. Steel South Works site to be transformed into quantum computing park Pierogi Fest grew from one vendor who sold out in an hour to a wall-to-wall crush of starch-seeking revelers, expects record attendance UPDATE: Valpo woman identified as deceased in fatal U.S. 20 crash Davich: Frankly, this is the best hot dog joint in the Region Big Lots closing stores in Chicagoland and Indiana, warns it may close all stores Region police release photo of wanted man Police seek help identifying 2 captured on video following home invasion 24-Hour Pierogi People: Annual Whiting festival elevates the dumpling to an artform Gas prices spike over $4 a gallon in Northwest Indiana after storms cause refinery power outage NWI Business Ins and Outs: Cajun Express, Fuerte Restaurant, Frankie's Philly Steak, The Waxi Taxi, Phenix Salon Suites and Boot Barn opening Pastor seeks to dump video of him allegedly battering child\n\nHarris' campaign has been vetting about a dozen potential running mates, according to people familiar with the search process. Shapiro and Arizona Sen. Mark Kelly are seen as among the front-runners, according to the people.\n\nThe campaign said Harris and her running mate will make stops next week in Philadelphia; western Wisconsin; Detroit; Raleigh, North Carolina; Savannah, Georgia; Phoenix and Las Vegas.\n\nIn the meantime, Harris advisers, led by former Attorney General Eric Holder, have been combing through reams of paperwork submitted by potential running mates, while the candidate herself is holding personal conversations with the finalists, according to a person familiar with the matter who spoke on the condition of anonymity to discuss details that haven't been made public.\n\nHarris, according to another person familiar with the matter, is seeking someone with executive experience who can also serve as a governing partner. Notions of a so-called short list have not stopped those on the Democrats' broader national bench from finding the spotlight.\n\n\"I'm not going to talk about the interactions I've had with the campaign,\" Illinois Gov. J.B. Pritzker declared recently on MSNBC. He added, though, \"Let's just say I'm aware that the vetting process is quite an in depth one.\" Then he listed his accomplishments, offering that he was the only Midwestern governor to raise his state minimum wage to $15 per hour.\n\nHarris would be the first woman, first Black woman and first person of South Asian descent to serve as president. Many Democrats have argued she should balance her ticket both demographically and politically.\n\nDems' VP list has notable differences\n\nShapiro, 51, is among the most popular U.S. governors, winning his 2022 election in a rout over a Trump-endorsed Republican. He's an outspoken supporter of abortion rights who has won three statewide elections in Pennsylvania. His speaking style draws comparisons to former President Barack Obama. But he has taken flak from the left for his support for Israel's war on Hamas, a private school voucher program and natural gas infrastructure.\n\nHis allies argue that he would help Harris win Pennsylvania, complicating if not blocking Trump's path to an Electoral College majority.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nLike all contenders, Shapiro sidesteps questions about the vetting process and stresses Harris should not be pressured. But he's mentioned more than once that he's known her for nearly two decades.\n\nBeshear stands out in a heavily Republican state. During his weekend stop in Georgia, he talked of winning votes in \"tough counties\" but emphasized liberal bona fides: \"I am a proud pro-union governor. I am a proud pro-choice governor. I am a proud public education governor. I am a proud pro-diversity governor.\"\n\nClosest in age to JD Vance among the Democrats' possibilities, Beshear openly mocks Trump's understudy for presenting himself as a son of Appalachia. \"I mean, there's a county that JD Vance says he's from in Kentucky - and I won it by 22 points last November,\" he said.\n\nBeshear and Shapiro were both state attorneys general, like Harris, before becoming governors. But their tenures did not overlap considerably with Harris' service in California. She worked more closely with North Carolina Gov. Roy Cooper during his days as an attorney general, but Cooper on Monday said he had opted not to be considered for vice president.\n\nMinnesota Gov. Tim Walz, 60, is a favorite of some progressives. He brings an atypical national political resume: He was a non-commissioned Army officer, public school teacher and state high school championship football coach before entering politics. Before being elected governor, he was one of the last white Democrats in Congress to represent a mostly rural, small-town House district -- a notable juxtaposition to Harris, the Bay Area Californian.\n\n\"She will make the best choice she's going to,\" Walz said Sunday on CNN, a day after Trump held a mass rally in St. Cloud, Minnesota. \"One way or another, she is going to win in November, and that's going to benefit everyone,\" Walz said, including \"a lot of those folks who were out in St. Cloud with the (former) president.\"\n\nKelly, 60, is the only top contender in Congress. He boasts an impressive military resume and experience as an astronaut. He has strong Latino support locally and solid relationships with Arizona officials along the U.S.-Mexico border. That balance could give him credibility on immigration policy as Republicans frame high numbers of migrant border crossings as a national crisis.\n\nBut Kelly has had to shore up his credentials with labor, a key Democratic faction. He took heat from union leaders because he was one of just a handful of Democrats who did not sponsor the Protecting the Right to Organize (PRO) Act, which would make it easier to organize workers. He said at the time he supported the goals but had concerns. Following pressure this month, he now says he would vote for the bill if it came up for a vote.\n\nEveryone has an opinion\n\nWhile Harris considers her choices, everyone seems to have an opinion.\n\nSteven Benjamin, the White House director of public engagement, laughed as he told reporters on Air Force One on Monday that his office has received thousands of recommendations from around the country.\n\nDonna Brazile, who managed Democrat Al Gore's presidential campaign in 2000 and was instrumental in urging Biden to pick Harris in 2020, said the selection process involves \"a lot of noise\" that underplays the complexity of the decision.\n\n\"The most important stage is what the lawyers will do to you,\" she said, with a laugh and emphasizing the seriousness. \"It's worse than a dental hygiene check. ... Before you get to suitability and other factors, before it gets to political people like me, they've done a forensic examination of your life.\"\n\nBarrow reported from Cumming, Georgia. Associated Press reporters Will Weissert in Washington, Gary Robertson in Raleigh, North Carolina; Marc Levy in Harrisburg, Pennsylvania; Bruce Schreiner in Frankfort, Kentucky; Jonathan J. Cooper in Phoenix; and Colleen Long aboard Air Force One contributed.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. {"uri": "8251650584", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-31", "time": "03:14:21", "dateTime": "2024-07-31T03:14:21Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-31T03:12:45Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7960784435272217, "url": "https://napavalleyregister.com/partners/ap/national/harris-is-planning-a-tour-of-battleground-states-next-week-with-her-yet-to-be/article_bdfcb42d-bb54-5621-85d1-0a6238046db8.html", "title": "Harris is planning a tour of battleground states next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate", "body": "Michigan Gov. Gretchen Whitmer meets with attendees during campaign event for Democratic presidential candidate Vice President Kamala Harris in Ambler, Pa., Monday, July 29, 2024.\n\nBy BILL BARROW, STEVE PEOPLES and ZEKE MILLER - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Vice President Kamala Harris is set to launch a battleground tour next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate, with stops in seven swing states stretching from Pennsylvania to Nevada, her campaign said Tuesday.\n\nThe planned tour is the latest sign of the whirlwind pace at which Harris has gone from President Joe Biden's supportive running mate to likely Democratic nominee ready to identify her No. 2 and take on Republican Donald Trump and Ohio Sen. JD Vance.\n\nHarris said Tuesday she hadn't made a decision yet on whom she will select.\n\nThose who are under consideration have been demonstrating a time-honored tradition: summertime auditions in which vice presidential contenders walk the line between open self-promotion and loyal advocacy for the potential boss.\n\nTo that end, Democrat Josh Shapiro told enthusiastic voters in suburban Philadelphia this week that Harris belongs in the White House -- and then reminded them of all he's done as governor of battleground Pennsylvania. Kentucky Gov. Andy Beshear, likewise, told voters in Georgia that Harris has the makings of \"a great president\" -- and then highlighted the elections he's won as a Democrat in Republican territory.\n\nPeople are also reading... Bankrupt owner of Napa Valley wineries announces 377 layoffs Drivers to soon get a taste of Napa's Soscol Junction roundabouts Vintage Wine Estates files for bankruptcy, sells Cosentino Winery in Napa Valley Growing pains: Violence-related NVUSD suspensions track with enrollment in middle schools Napa's Soscol Junction makes its partial debut. A Register reporter tests it. Register Explainer: A deeper look at Belia Ramos vs. Debra Dommen Napa Valley Distillery plans to rebuild, reopen after fiery crash Interconnected relationships at heart of restraining order case involving Napa supervisor and wine executive Restaurant owners, workers urge action against unpermitted food vending in Napa Update: Highway 128 reopens in Napa County after submerged vehicle found Napa County judge denies resentencing in 2014 Kayleigh Slusher death Letter: Stop tainting the name of Alta Heights 'No place in our community,' police and DA say after antisemitic flyers scattered in Napa New website supporting Napa day care goes live after controversial site taken down Update: Distillery building damaged by fire after Highway 29 crash in Napa\n\nHarris' campaign has been vetting about a dozen potential running mates, according to people familiar with the search process. Shapiro and Arizona Sen. Mark Kelly are seen as among the front-runners, according to the people.\n\nThe campaign said Harris and her running mate will make stops next week in Philadelphia; western Wisconsin; Detroit; Raleigh, North Carolina; Savannah, Georgia; Phoenix and Las Vegas.\n\nIn the meantime, Harris advisers, led by former Attorney General Eric Holder, have been combing through reams of paperwork submitted by potential running mates, while the candidate herself is holding personal conversations with the finalists, according to a person familiar with the matter who spoke on the condition of anonymity to discuss details that haven't been made public.\n\nHarris, according to another person familiar with the matter, is seeking someone with executive experience who can also serve as a governing partner. Notions of a so-called short list have not stopped those on the Democrats' broader national bench from finding the spotlight.\n\n\"I'm not going to talk about the interactions I've had with the campaign,\" Illinois Gov. J.B. Pritzker declared recently on MSNBC. He added, though, \"Let's just say I'm aware that the vetting process is quite an in depth one.\" Then he listed his accomplishments, offering that he was the only Midwestern governor to raise his state minimum wage to $15 per hour.\n\nHarris would be the first woman, first Black woman and first person of South Asian descent to serve as president. Many Democrats have argued she should balance her ticket both demographically and politically.\n\nDems' VP list has notable differences\n\nShapiro, 51, is among the most popular U.S. governors, winning his 2022 election in a rout over a Trump-endorsed Republican. He's an outspoken supporter of abortion rights who has won three statewide elections in Pennsylvania. His speaking style draws comparisons to former President Barack Obama. But he has taken flak from the left for his support for Israel's war on Hamas, a private school voucher program and natural gas infrastructure.\n\nHis allies argue that he would help Harris win Pennsylvania, complicating if not blocking Trump's path to an Electoral College majority.\n\nLike all contenders, Shapiro sidesteps questions about the vetting process and stresses Harris should not be pressured. But he's mentioned more than once that he's known her for nearly two decades.\n\nBeshear stands out in a heavily Republican state. During his weekend stop in Georgia, he talked of winning votes in \"tough counties\" but emphasized liberal bona fides: \"I am a proud pro-union governor. I am a proud pro-choice governor. I am a proud public education governor. I am a proud pro-diversity governor.\"\n\nClosest in age to JD Vance among the Democrats' possibilities, Beshear openly mocks Trump's understudy for presenting himself as a son of Appalachia. \"I mean, there's a county that JD Vance says he's from in Kentucky - and I won it by 22 points last November,\" he said.\n\nBeshear and Shapiro were both state attorneys general, like Harris, before becoming governors. But their tenures did not overlap considerably with Harris' service in California. She worked more closely with North Carolina Gov. Roy Cooper during his days as an attorney general, but Cooper on Monday said he had opted not to be considered for vice president.\n\nMinnesota Gov. Tim Walz, 60, is a favorite of some progressives. He brings an atypical national political resume: He was a non-commissioned Army officer, public school teacher and state high school championship football coach before entering politics. Before being elected governor, he was one of the last white Democrats in Congress to represent a mostly rural, small-town House district -- a notable juxtaposition to Harris, the Bay Area Californian.\n\n\"She will make the best choice she's going to,\" Walz said Sunday on CNN, a day after Trump held a mass rally in St. Cloud, Minnesota. \"One way or another, she is going to win in November, and that's going to benefit everyone,\" Walz said, including \"a lot of those folks who were out in St. Cloud with the (former) president.\"\n\nKelly, 60, is the only top contender in Congress. He boasts an impressive military resume and experience as an astronaut. He has strong Latino support locally and solid relationships with Arizona officials along the U.S.-Mexico border. That balance could give him credibility on immigration policy as Republicans frame high numbers of migrant border crossings as a national crisis.\n\nBut Kelly has had to shore up his credentials with labor, a key Democratic faction. He took heat from union leaders because he was one of just a handful of Democrats who did not sponsor the Protecting the Right to Organize (PRO) Act, which would make it easier to organize workers. He said at the time he supported the goals but had concerns. Following pressure this month, he now says he would vote for the bill if it came up for a vote.\n\nEveryone has an opinion\n\nWhile Harris considers her choices, everyone seems to have an opinion.\n\nSteven Benjamin, the White House director of public engagement, laughed as he told reporters on Air Force One on Monday that his office has received thousands of recommendations from around the country.\n\nDonna Brazile, who managed Democrat Al Gore's presidential campaign in 2000 and was instrumental in urging Biden to pick Harris in 2020, said the selection process involves \"a lot of noise\" that underplays the complexity of the decision.\n\n\"The most important stage is what the lawyers will do to you,\" she said, with a laugh and emphasizing the seriousness. \"It's worse than a dental hygiene check. ... Before you get to suitability and other factors, before it gets to political people like me, they've done a forensic examination of your life.\"\n\nBarrow reported from Cumming, Georgia. Associated Press reporters Will Weissert in Washington, Gary Robertson in Raleigh, North Carolina; Marc Levy in Harrisburg, Pennsylvania; Bruce Schreiner in Frankfort, Kentucky; Jonathan J. Cooper in Phoenix; and Colleen Long aboard Air Force One contributed.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. {"uri": "8251552662", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-31", "time": "01:10:04", "dateTime": "2024-07-31T01:10:04Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-31T01:09:12Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7921568751335144, "url": "https://lasvegassun.com/news/2024/jul/30/the-democratic-contest-to-be-harris-running-mate-w/", "title": "The Democratic contest to be Harris' running mate will likely be decided in the next week", "body": "John Bazemore / AP\n\nVice President Kamala Harris speaks during a campaign rally, Tuesday, July 30, 2024, in Atlanta.\n\nBy Bill Barrow and Steve Peoples, Associated Press\n\nTuesday, July 30, 2024 | 5:59 p.m.\n\nAMBLER, Pa. -- Democrat Josh Shapiro had a dual message for enthusiastic voters in suburban Philadelphia this week, telling them Kamala Harris belongs in the White House -- and then reminding them of all he's done as governor of battleground Pennsylvania. Kentucky Gov. Andy Beshear, likewise, told voters in Georgia that Harris has the makings of \"a great president\" -- and then highlighted the elections he's won as a Democrat in Republican territory.\n\nThe two governors were demonstrating a time-honored tradition in presidential campaigns: Summertime auditions from vice presidential contenders who walk the line between open self-promotion and loyal advocacy for the potential boss.\n\nVice President Harris, the likely Democratic nominee, appears intent on making a choice that she's comfortable with personally and that can expand her electoral appeal in a matter of days. Her campaign has been vetting about a dozen potential running mates, according to people familiar with the search process. Shapiro and Arizona Sen. Mark Kelly are seen as among the front-runners, according to the people.\n\nThree people familiar with the vice president's plans said Tuesday that she and her yet-to-be-named running mate would begin traveling to battleground states next week -- suggesting that a decision could be coming soon. The people said they didn't know who her pick would be or which states Harris would visit, although one said Savannah, Georgia, was on the itinerary. They spoke on the condition of anonymity to discuss details that haven't been made public.\n\nIn the meantime, Harris advisers, led by former Attorney General Eric Holder, have been combing through reams of paperwork submitted by potential running mates, while the candidate herself is holding personal conversations with the finalists, according to a person familiar with the matter.\n\nHarris, according to another person familiar with the matter, is seeking someone with executive experience who can also serve as a governing partner. Notions of a so-called short list have not stopped those on the Democrats' broader national bench from finding the spotlight.\n\n\"I'm not going to talk about the interactions I've had with the campaign,\" Illinois Gov. J.B. Pritzker declared recently on MSNBC. He added, though: \"Let's just say I'm aware that the vetting process is quite an in depth one.\" Then he listed his accomplishments, offering that he was the only Midwestern governor to raise his state minimum wage to $15 per hour.\n\nMichigan Gov. Gretchen Whitmer, once held out as an ideal nominee if Biden bailed out, has said, more or less, that she's not a contender. But she appeared Monday with Shapiro in Pennsylvania and mused on MSNBC last week that \"two women on the ticket would be exciting.\"\n\nHarris would be the first woman, first Black woman and first person of South Asian descent to serve as president. Many Democrats have argued she should balance her ticket both demographically and politically.\n\nDems' VP list has notable differences\n\nShapiro, 51, is among the most popular U.S. governors, winning his 2022 election in a rout over a Trump-endorsed Republican. He's an outspoken supporter of abortion rights who has won three statewide elections in Pennsylvania. His speaking style draws comparisons to former President Barack Obama. But he has taken flak from the left for his support for Israel's war on Hamas, a private school voucher program and natural gas infrastructure.\n\nHis allies argue that he would help Harris win Pennsylvania, complicating if not blocking Republican Donald Trump's path to an Electoral College majority.\n\nLike all contenders, Shapiro sidesteps questions about the vetting process and stresses Harris should not be pressured. But he's mentioned more than once that he's known her for nearly two decades.\n\nBeshear stands out in a heavily Republican state. During his weekend stop in Georgia, he talked of winning votes in \"tough counties\" but emphasized liberal bona fides: \"I am a proud pro-union governor. I am a proud pro-choice governor. I am a proud public education governor. I am a proud pro-diversity governor.\"\n\nClosest in age to JD Vance among the Democrats' possibilities, Beshear openly mocks Trump's understudy for presenting himself as a son of Appalachia. \"I mean, there's a county that JD Vance says he's from in Kentucky - and I won it by 22 points last November,\" he said.\n\nBack home in Frankfort recently, Beshear played down the importance of being from a battleground, saying, \"About every successful ticket going back to 2000 did not have someone in a swing state.\"\n\nOf course, sometimes the spotlight can produce mistakes. Twice in Georgia, Beshear mispronounced Harris' first name as \"Kah-MAH-lah,\" rather than the correct \"KAH-mah-lah.\"\n\nBeshear and Shapiro were both state attorneys general, like Harris, before becoming governors. But their tenures did not overlap considerably with Harris' service in California. She worked more closely with North Carolina Gov. Roy Cooper during his days as an attorney general, but Cooper on Monday said he had opted not to be considered for vice president.\n\nMinnesota Gov. Tim Walz, 60, is a favorite of some progressives. He brings an atypical national political resume: He was a non-commissioned Army officer, public school teacher and state high school championship football coach before entering politics. Before being elected governor, he was one of the last white Democrats in Congress to represent a mostly rural, small-town House district -- a notable juxtaposition for Harris, the Bay Area Californian.\n\n\"She will make the best choice she's going to,\" Walz said Sunday on CNN, a day after Trump held a mass rally in St. Cloud, Minnesota. \"One way or another, she is going to win in November, and that's going to benefit everyone,\" Walz said, including \"a lot of those folks who were out in St. Cloud with the (former) president.\"\n\nKelly, 60, is the only top contender in Congress. He boasts an impressive military resume and experience as an astronaut. He has strong Latino support locally and solid relationships with Arizona officials along the U.S.-Mexico border. That balance could give him credibility on immigration policy as Republicans frame high numbers of migrant border crossings as a national crisis.\n\nBut Kelly has had to shore up his credentials with labor, a key Democratic faction. He took heat from union leaders because he was one of just a handful of Democrats who did not sponsor the Protecting the Right to Organize (PRO) Act, which would make it easier to organize workers. He said at the time he supported the goals but had concerns. Following pressure this month, he now says he would vote for the bill if it came up for a vote.\n\nEveryone has an opinion\n\nHarris is expected to announce her pick in time for Democratic delegates to ratify her decision in a virtual nominating vote that could conclude by Aug. 7. Whatever her timetable, the media and campaign circuit is allowing plenty of Democrats additional time in the spotlight.\n\nIn the meantime, everyone seems to have an opinion.\n\nSteven Benjamin, the White House director of public engagement, laughed as he told reporters on Air Force One on Monday that his office has received thousands of recommendations from around the country.\n\nDonna Brazile, who managed Democrat Al Gore's presidential campaign in 2000 and was instrumental in urging Biden to pick Harris in 2020, said the selection process involves \"a lot of noise\" that underplays the complexity of the decision.\n\n\"The most important stage is what the lawyers will do to you,\" she said, with a laugh and emphasizing the seriousness. \"It's worse than a dental hygiene check. ... Before you get to suitability and other factors, before it gets to political people like me, they've done a forensic examination of your life.\"\n\n___\n\nBarrow reported from Cumming, Georgia. Gretchen Whitmer meets with attendees during campaign event for Democratic presidential candidate Vice President Kamala Harris in Ambler, Pa., Monday, July 29, 2024.\n\nBy BILL BARROW, STEVE PEOPLES and ZEKE MILLER - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Vice President Kamala Harris is set to launch a battleground tour next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate, with stops in seven swing states stretching from Pennsylvania to Nevada, her campaign said Tuesday.\n\nThe planned tour is the latest sign of the whirlwind pace at which Harris has gone from President Joe Biden's supportive running mate to likely Democratic nominee ready to identify her No. 2 and take on Republican Donald Trump and Ohio Sen. JD Vance.\n\nHarris said Tuesday she hadn't made a decision yet on whom she will select.\n\nThose who are under consideration have been demonstrating a time-honored tradition: summertime auditions in which vice presidential contenders walk the line between open self-promotion and loyal advocacy for the potential boss.\n\nTo that end, Democrat Josh Shapiro told enthusiastic voters in suburban Philadelphia this week that Harris belongs in the White House -- and then reminded them of all he's done as governor of battleground Pennsylvania. Kentucky Gov. Andy Beshear, likewise, told voters in Georgia that Harris has the makings of \"a great president\" -- and then highlighted the elections he's won as a Democrat in Republican territory.\n\nPeople are also reading... The Big Tri City Fair looks to bring fun and entertainment HIGH SCHOOL BASKETBALL: Hart steps down at Honaker, not seeing commitment in offseason OLYMPICS: Johnson City-born Brody Malone helps U.S. get bronze in men's gymnastics team competition PREP SPORTS: VHSL leaders hold public meeting in Abingdon Antiques showcased at Virginia Highlands Festival HIGH SCHOOL FOOTBALL: Versatile Elliott to see plenty of the field for Vikings Owens will be missed at annual Creeper Trail Ride to End Cancer Only the name has changed; 'The Classic' expected to become bigger and better BHC SOFTBALL PLAYER OF YEAR: Muncy completes stellar career with state title at Rye Cove Drug probe leads to arrests, seizure of $1.9M of meth, fentanyl MLB: Evan Carter's back injury among Rangers concerns Badcock Furniture closing all its retail locations This theater season brings friendship, camaraderie and nostalgia to the stage PRO GOLF: Runner-up for Connor Creasy (Abingdon) in PGA Americas Tour tourney in Ottawa Candidate Cao meets with Republicans in Abingdon\n\nHarris' campaign has been vetting about a dozen potential running mates, according to people familiar with the search process. Shapiro and Arizona Sen. Mark Kelly are seen as among the front-runners, according to the people.\n\nThe campaign said Harris and her running mate will make stops next week in Philadelphia; western Wisconsin; Detroit; Raleigh, North Carolina; Savannah, Georgia; Phoenix and Las Vegas.\n\nIn the meantime, Harris advisers, led by former Attorney General Eric Holder, have been combing through reams of paperwork submitted by potential running mates, while the candidate herself is holding personal conversations with the finalists, according to a person familiar with the matter who spoke on the condition of anonymity to discuss details that haven't been made public.\n\nHarris, according to another person familiar with the matter, is seeking someone with executive experience who can also serve as a governing partner. Notions of a so-called short list have not stopped those on the Democrats' broader national bench from finding the spotlight.\n\n\"I'm not going to talk about the interactions I've had with the campaign,\" Illinois Gov. J.B. Pritzker declared recently on MSNBC. He added, though, \"Let's just say I'm aware that the vetting process is quite an in depth one.\" Then he listed his accomplishments, offering that he was the only Midwestern governor to raise his state minimum wage to $15 per hour.\n\nHarris would be the first woman, first Black woman and first person of South Asian descent to serve as president. Many Democrats have argued she should balance her ticket both demographically and politically.\n\nDems' VP list has notable differences\n\nShapiro, 51, is among the most popular U.S. governors, winning his 2022 election in a rout over a Trump-endorsed Republican. He's an outspoken supporter of abortion rights who has won three statewide elections in Pennsylvania. His speaking style draws comparisons to former President Barack Obama. But he has taken flak from the left for his support for Israel's war on Hamas, a private school voucher program and natural gas infrastructure.\n\nHis allies argue that he would help Harris win Pennsylvania, complicating if not blocking Trump's path to an Electoral College majority.\n\nLike all contenders, Shapiro sidesteps questions about the vetting process and stresses Harris should not be pressured. But he's mentioned more than once that he's known her for nearly two decades.\n\nBeshear stands out in a heavily Republican state. During his weekend stop in Georgia, he talked of winning votes in \"tough counties\" but emphasized liberal bona fides: \"I am a proud pro-union governor. I am a proud pro-choice governor. I am a proud public education governor. I am a proud pro-diversity governor.\"\n\nClosest in age to JD Vance among the Democrats' possibilities, Beshear openly mocks Trump's understudy for presenting himself as a son of Appalachia. \"I mean, there's a county that JD Vance says he's from in Kentucky - and I won it by 22 points last November,\" he said.\n\nBeshear and Shapiro were both state attorneys general, like Harris, before becoming governors. But their tenures did not overlap considerably with Harris' service in California. She worked more closely with North Carolina Gov. Roy Cooper during his days as an attorney general, but Cooper on Monday said he had opted not to be considered for vice president.\n\nMinnesota Gov. Tim Walz, 60, is a favorite of some progressives. He brings an atypical national political resume: He was a non-commissioned Army officer, public school teacher and state high school championship football coach before entering politics. Before being elected governor, he was one of the last white Democrats in Congress to represent a mostly rural, small-town House district -- a notable juxtaposition to Harris, the Bay Area Californian.\n\n\"She will make the best choice she's going to,\" Walz said Sunday on CNN, a day after Trump held a mass rally in St. Cloud, Minnesota. \"One way or another, she is going to win in November, and that's going to benefit everyone,\" Walz said, including \"a lot of those folks who were out in St. Cloud with the (former) president.\"\n\nKelly, 60, is the only top contender in Congress. He boasts an impressive military resume and experience as an astronaut. He has strong Latino support locally and solid relationships with Arizona officials along the U.S.-Mexico border. That balance could give him credibility on immigration policy as Republicans frame high numbers of migrant border crossings as a national crisis.\n\nBut Kelly has had to shore up his credentials with labor, a key Democratic faction. He took heat from union leaders because he was one of just a handful of Democrats who did not sponsor the Protecting the Right to Organize (PRO) Act, which would make it easier to organize workers. He said at the time he supported the goals but had concerns. Following pressure this month, he now says he would vote for the bill if it came up for a vote.\n\nEveryone has an opinion\n\nWhile Harris considers her choices, everyone seems to have an opinion.\n\nSteven Benjamin, the White House director of public engagement, laughed as he told reporters on Air Force One on Monday that his office has received thousands of recommendations from around the country.\n\nDonna Brazile, who managed Democrat Al Gore's presidential campaign in 2000 and was instrumental in urging Biden to pick Harris in 2020, said the selection process involves \"a lot of noise\" that underplays the complexity of the decision.\n\n\"The most important stage is what the lawyers will do to you,\" she said, with a laugh and emphasizing the seriousness. \"It's worse than a dental hygiene check. ... Before you get to suitability and other factors, before it gets to political people like me, they've done a forensic examination of your life.\"\n\nBarrow reported from Cumming, Georgia. {"uri": "8251627042", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-31", "time": "02:45:52", "dateTime": "2024-07-31T02:45:52Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-31T02:45:04Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7803921699523926, "url": "https://www.stltoday.com/news/state-and-regional/illinois/harris-is-planning-a-tour-of-battleground-states-next-week-with-her-yet-to-be/article_161e9eb2-6b27-587f-b9fa-289f078a5517.html", "title": "Harris is planning a tour of battleground states next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate", "body": "By BILL BARROW, STEVE PEOPLES and ZEKE MILLER - Associated Press By BILL BARROW, STEVE PEOPLES and ZEKE MILLER - Associated Press\n\nWASHINGTON (AP) -- Vice President Kamala Harris is set to launch a battleground tour next week with her yet-to-be-named running mate, with stops in seven swing states stretching from Pennsylvania to Nevada, her campaign said Tuesday.\n\nThe planned tour is the latest sign of the whirlwind pace at which Harris has gone from President Joe Biden's supportive running mate to likely Democratic nominee ready to identify her No. 2 and take on Republican Donald Trump and Ohio Sen. JD Vance.\n\nHarris said Tuesday she hadn't made a decision yet on whom she will select.\n\nThose who are under consideration have been demonstrating a time-honored tradition: summertime auditions in which vice presidential contenders walk the line between open self-promotion and loyal advocacy for the potential boss.\n\nTo that end, Democrat Josh Shapiro told enthusiastic voters in suburban Philadelphia this week that Harris belongs in the White House -- and then reminded them of all he's done as governor of battleground Pennsylvania. Kentucky Gov. Andy Beshear, likewise, told voters in Georgia that Harris has the makings of \"a great president\" -- and then highlighted the elections he's won as a Democrat in Republican territory.\n\nWho's Donald Trump's pick for Missouri governor? He's got three. Inside the ouster: Why the St. Louis Public Schools leader was pushed out after 1 year St. Louis Public Schools superintendent Keisha Scarlett is out, replaced with interim chief Ranch in Wildwood is sanctuary for abused miniature horses Cardinals land starter Erick Fedde and Tommy Pham, but trade Tommy Edman in 3-team deal Mercy paid former exec $1 million in wake of 'sexual exploitation' allegations Lawsuit accuses Archdiocese of St. Louis of allowing clergy to abuse children Messenger: He fixed up his mobile home. He made it too nice. The city wants him out. Mine collapse leaves Alton golf course shut down, paying six figures a month Hair relaxer ingredients tied to cancer risk, say lawsuits and FDA. St. Louis stylists offer options. St. Louis County lawsuit names Omaha archbishop among sexual abusers. He denies the claim. Editorial: Bell has our enthusiastic endorsement for Missouri's 1st Congressional District 'He outlasted all of them': How 13-year-old gelding succeeds as one of nation's oldest racehorses Tommy Edman reflects on time in St. Louis after an 'abrupt ending': Cardinals Extra Goold's chat: What 3 ways could Cardinals assert themselves as contenders? Is trade only 1?\n\nHarris' campaign has been vetting about a dozen potential running mates, according to people familiar with the search process. Shapiro and Arizona Sen. Mark Kelly are seen as among the front-runners, according to the people.\n\nThe campaign said Harris and her running mate will make stops next week in Philadelphia; western Wisconsin; Detroit; Raleigh, North Carolina; Savannah, Georgia; Phoenix and Las Vegas.\n\nIn the meantime, Harris advisers, led by former Attorney General Eric Holder, have been combing through reams of paperwork submitted by potential running mates, while the candidate herself is holding personal conversations with the finalists, according to a person familiar with the matter who spoke on the condition of anonymity to discuss details that haven't been made public.\n\nHarris, according to another person familiar with the matter, is seeking someone with executive experience who can also serve as a governing partner. Notions of a so-called short list have not stopped those on the Democrats' broader national bench from finding the spotlight.\n\n\"I'm not going to talk about the interactions I've had with the campaign,\" Illinois Gov. J.B. Pritzker declared recently on MSNBC. He added, though, \"Let's just say I'm aware that the vetting process is quite an in depth one.\" Then he listed his accomplishments, offering that he was the only Midwestern governor to raise his state minimum wage to $15 per hour.\n\nHarris would be the first woman, first Black woman and first person of South Asian descent to serve as president. Many Democrats have argued she should balance her ticket both demographically and politically.\n\nDems' VP list has notable differences\n\nShapiro, 51, is among the most popular U.S. governors, winning his 2022 election in a rout over a Trump-endorsed Republican. He's an outspoken supporter of abortion rights who has won three statewide elections in Pennsylvania. His speaking style draws comparisons to former President Barack Obama. But he has taken flak from the left for his support for Israel's war on Hamas, a private school voucher program and natural gas infrastructure.\n\nHis allies argue that he would help Harris win Pennsylvania, complicating if not blocking Trump's path to an Electoral College majority.\n\nLike all contenders, Shapiro sidesteps questions about the vetting process and stresses Harris should not be pressured. But he's mentioned more than once that he's known her for nearly two decades.\n\nBeshear stands out in a heavily Republican state. During his weekend stop in Georgia, he talked of winning votes in \"tough counties\" but emphasized liberal bona fides: \"I am a proud pro-union governor. I am a proud pro-choice governor. I am a proud public education governor. I am a proud pro-diversity governor.\"\n\nClosest in age to JD Vance among the Democrats' possibilities, Beshear openly mocks Trump's understudy for presenting himself as a son of Appalachia. \"I mean, there's a county that JD Vance says he's from in Kentucky - and I won it by 22 points last November,\" he said.\n\nBeshear and Shapiro were both state attorneys general, like Harris, before becoming governors. But their tenures did not overlap considerably with Harris' service in California. She worked more closely with North Carolina Gov. Roy Cooper during his days as an attorney general, but Cooper on Monday said he had opted not to be considered for vice president.\n\nMinnesota Gov. Tim Walz, 60, is a favorite of some progressives. He brings an atypical national political resume: He was a non-commissioned Army officer, public school teacher and state high school championship football coach before entering politics. Before being elected governor, he was one of the last white Democrats in Congress to represent a mostly rural, small-town House district -- a notable juxtaposition to Harris, the Bay Area Californian.\n\n\"She will make the best choice she's going to,\" Walz said Sunday on CNN, a day after Trump held a mass rally in St. Cloud, Minnesota. \"One way or another, she is going to win in November, and that's going to benefit everyone,\" Walz said, including \"a lot of those folks who were out in St. Cloud with the (former) president.\"\n\nKelly, 60, is the only top contender in Congress. He boasts an impressive military resume and experience as an astronaut. He has strong Latino support locally and solid relationships with Arizona officials along the U.S.-Mexico border. That balance could give him credibility on immigration policy as Republicans frame high numbers of migrant border crossings as a national crisis.\n\nBut Kelly has had to shore up his credentials with labor, a key Democratic faction. He took heat from union leaders because he was one of just a handful of Democrats who did not sponsor the Protecting the Right to Organize (PRO) Act, which would make it easier to organize workers. He said at the time he supported the goals but had concerns. Following pressure this month, he now says he would vote for the bill if it came up for a vote.\n\nEveryone has an opinion\n\nWhile Harris considers her choices, everyone seems to have an opinion.\n\nSteven Benjamin, the White House director of public engagement, laughed as he told reporters on Air Force One on Monday that his office has received thousands of recommendations from around the country.\n\nDonna Brazile, who managed Democrat Al Gore's presidential campaign in 2000 and was instrumental in urging Biden to pick Harris in 2020, said the selection process involves \"a lot of noise\" that underplays the complexity of the decision.\n\n\"The most important stage is what the lawyers will do to you,\" she said, with a laugh and emphasizing the seriousness. \"It's worse than a dental hygiene check. ... Before you get to suitability and other factors, before it gets to political people like me, they've done a forensic examination of your life.\"\n\nBarrow reported from Cumming, Georgia. Associated Press reporters Will Weissert in Washington, Gary Robertson in Raleigh, North Carolina; Marc Levy in Harrisburg, Pennsylvania; Bruce Schreiner in Frankfort, Kentucky; Jonathan J. Cooper in Phoenix; and Colleen Long aboard Air Force One contributed.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved.
